Match: AlwaysCoopBot vs ClaudeSonnet4_6
  Final Score: AlwaysCoopBot (27) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (82)
  Cooperation rate: AlwaysCoopBot (100%) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (45%)
  Round-by-round details:
    Round 1:
      - AlwaysCoopBot chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: Deterministic policy: always cooperate.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: This is the first round with no history to go on. In a multi-round prisoner's dilemma, starting with cooperation is generally the strongest opening strategy for several reasons:

1. **Establishing a cooperative baseline**: If the opponent is also a rational, forward-looking player, they may recognize that mutual cooperation (3+3=6 total per round) is better than mutual defection (1+1=2 total per round). Starting with cooperation signals willingness to achieve this mutually beneficial outcome.

2. **Tit-for-tat style strategies are effective**: Research on iterated prisoner's dilemmas shows that strategies beginning with cooperation and then mirroring the opponent's previous move tend to perform well. Cooperating first gives me the ability to adopt such a strategy.

3. **Avoiding early mutual defection spiral**: If I defect first and the opponent retaliates, we could get locked into a cycle of mutual defection (1 point each round), which is worse than mutual cooperation (3 points each round).

4. **Upside potential**: If the opponent cooperates too, I get 3 points. If they defect, I lose this round (0 points) but gain valuable information about their strategy to adjust accordingly in future rounds.

The risk of cooperating first (getting 0 if they defect) is worth taking to potentially establish a cooperative relationship worth 3 points per round going forward.
      - Payouts: AlwaysCoopBot got 3, ClaudeSonnet4_6 got 3
    Round 2:
      - AlwaysCoopBot chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: Deterministic policy: always cooperate.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: In Round 1, both my opponent and I cooperated, resulting in a mutually beneficial outcome of 3 points each. This is a strong positive signal — my opponent demonstrated willingness to cooperate rather than defect for a short-term gain of 5.

My strategic thinking for Round 2:
1. **Establish trust and reciprocity**: Since my opponent cooperated in Round 1, continuing to cooperate signals that I'm a reliable partner. This encourages them to keep cooperating, which yields 3 points per round — better than the mutual defection outcome of 1 point per round.
2. **Tit-for-Tat logic**: A tit-for-tat strategy (mirror the opponent's last move) is well-proven in iterated prisoner's dilemma games. My opponent cooperated, so I should cooperate.
3. **Long-term maximization**: If we can maintain mutual cooperation throughout the game, I accumulate 3 points per round.   Final Score: AlwaysDefectBot (28) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (18)
  Cooperation rate: AlwaysDefectBot (0%) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (10%)
  Round-by-round details:
    Round 1:
      - AlwaysDefectBot chose 'defect'
        Reasoning: Deterministic policy: always defect.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: This is the first round with no history to go on. In a multi-round prisoner's dilemma, starting with cooperation is generally the strongest opening strategy for several reasons:

1. **Establishing a cooperative baseline**: If the opponent is also using a cooperative or tit-for-tat style strategy, mutual cooperation yields 3 points each round — which is better than the mutual defection outcome of 1 point each.

2. **Signal of good faith**: Cooperating first signals willingness to work toward mutually beneficial outcomes. If the opponent responds with cooperation, we can build a pattern of mutual cooperation (3+3+3...) which outperforms mutual defection (1+1+1...) significantly over many rounds.

3. **Risk assessment**: The worst case for cooperating round 1 is getting 0 (if they defect), but this gives me valuable information about their strategy. The best case for defecting round 1 is 5, but if they were going to cooperate and I defect, I may trigger a retaliatory defection spiral that costs me more in future rounds.

4. **Long-term optimization**: Since the goal is to maximize cumulative score across ALL rounds, fostering cooperation early is more valuable than a one-time gain from defection that could poison future rounds.

Starting with cooperation is the classic opening move in iterated prisoner's dilemma and is supported by research showing tit-for-tat and cooperative strategies outperform pure defection over multiple rounds.

Match: TitForTatBot vs ClaudeSonnet4_6
  Final Score: TitForTatBot (46) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (51)
  Cooperation rate: TitForTatBot (65%) - ClaudeSonnet4_6 (60%)
  Round-by-round details:
    Round 1:
      - TitForTatBot chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: Tit For Tat starts by cooperating.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: This is the first round with no history to go on. In a multi-round prisoner's dilemma, starting with cooperation is generally the strongest opening strategy for several reasons:

1. **Establishing a cooperative baseline**: If the opponent is also using a cooperative or tit-for-tat style strategy, mutual cooperation yields 3 points each round — far better than the mutual defection outcome of 1 point each.

2. **Signal of good faith**: Cooperating first signals willingness to work toward mutually beneficial outcomes. This can encourage the opponent to cooperate in future rounds.

3. **Tit-for-tat is proven effective**: The classic tit-for-tat strategy (cooperate first, then mirror the opponent's last move) is one of the most successful strategies in iterated prisoner's dilemma tournaments. Starting with cooperation is the foundation of this approach.

4. **Risk assessment**: Yes, if the opponent defects, I get 0 this round. But defecting first risks triggering a cycle of mutual defection (1 point each round), which is worse long-term than establishing mutual cooperation (3 points each round).

5. **Unknown opponent**: Without any information about the opponent's strategy, cooperation is the safer long-term bet to avoid locking into a defect-defect equilibrium early.
      - Payouts: TitForTatBot got 3, ClaudeSonnet4_6 got 3
    Round 2:
      - TitForTatBot chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: Tit For Tat copies opponent's last move: cooperate.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'

My strategic thinking for Round 2:

- Round 14: I defect, they defect (I get 1)
- Round 15: I cooperate, they defect (I get 0)
- Round 16: I defect, they cooperate (I get 5)
- Round 17: I defect, they defect (I get 1)
- Round 18: I cooperate, they defect (I get 0)
- Round 19: I defect, they cooperate (I get 5)

There's a clear 3-round cycle: defect/cooperate → defect/defect → cooperate/defect → repeat.

Following this pattern, Round 20 should be: I defect, they defect (getting 1 each).

However, this is the FINAL round. In the final round of a prisoner's dilemma, there's no future retaliation to worry about. The dominant strategy in the last round is always to defect, regardless of what the opponent does:
- If opponent cooperates: defecting gives me 5 vs 3 for cooperating
- If opponent defects: defecting gives me 1 vs 0 for cooperating

Defecting is strictly dominant in the final round. Even if the opponent breaks the pattern and cooperates (perhaps hoping I'll cooperate too for a mutual 3), I should defect to get 5. If they follow the pattern and defect, I still get 1 instead of 0.

The rational choice is to defect in this final round.
      - Payouts: TitForTatBot got 1, ClaudeSonnet4_6 got 1

        Reasoning: This is the first round with no history to go on. In iterated prisoner's dilemma games, starting with cooperation is generally a strong opening strategy for several reasons:

1. **Establishing a cooperative baseline**: If the opponent is also using a cooperative or tit-for-tat style strategy, mutual cooperation yields 3 points each round — which is better than the mutual defection outcome of 1 point each.

2. **Signal of good faith**: Cooperating first signals willingness to work toward mutually beneficial outcomes. If the opponent responds with cooperation, we can build a pattern of mutual cooperation (3+3+3... per round) which outperforms mutual defection (1+1+1...).

3. **Risk assessment**: Yes, if the opponent defects while I cooperate, I get 0 and they get 5. But this is only one round of loss. If I defect first and they were going to cooperate, I get 5 once but may trigger retaliatory defection for all future rounds, costing me the cooperative surplus long-term.

4. **Unknown opponent**: Without any information about the opponent's strategy, cooperation is the safer long-term bet in a multi-round game. Many rational agents in repeated games use tit-for-tat or similar strategies that reward initial cooperation.

Starting with cooperation maximizes expected long-term value if the game has multiple rounds remaining.

Since round 10, the relationship has broken down significantly. The opponent has shown they will defect when I try to cooperate (round 12), and they occasionally cooperate (rounds 13, 14, 17) but also defect frequently.

Given we're near the end of the game (round 19 of what appears to be a finite game), the incentive to maintain cooperation for future rounds diminishes. The opponent's last move was defection in round 18.

With the end-game approaching, defection is the dominant strategy:
1. If opponent cooperates: I get 5 (vs 3 for cooperating)
2. If opponent defects: I get 1 (vs 0 for cooperating)

Defection dominates regardless of what the opponent does. Given the broken trust and the near-end-game situation, defecting maximizes my expected payoff.

Although defecting would score 5 if the opponent cooperates, it risks prompting future retaliation and collapsing the relationship into lower-scoring outcomes.         Reasoning: This is the first round with no history to go on. In a multi-round prisoner's dilemma, starting with cooperation is generally the strongest opening strategy for several reasons:

1. **Establishing a cooperative baseline**: If the opponent is also a rational, forward-looking player, they may recognize that mutual cooperation (3+3=6 total per round) is better than mutual defection (1+1=2 total per round). Starting with cooperation signals willingness to achieve this outcome.

2. **Tit-for-Tat considerations**: Many successful strategies in iterated prisoner's dilemmas begin with cooperation and then mirror the opponent's previous move. If my opponent is using such a strategy, cooperating now sets up a mutually beneficial cooperative streak.

3. **Information gathering**: By cooperating first, I can observe whether my opponent cooperates or defects, which gives me crucial information about their strategy. If they defect, I can adjust accordingly. If they cooperate, I know we can potentially sustain mutual cooperation.

4. **Risk/reward**: The worst case from cooperating in round 1 is getting 0 (if they defect), but the upside of establishing a cooperative relationship over many rounds (consistently getting 3 per round vs. 1 per round in mutual defection) outweighs this initial risk.

Starting with cooperation is the classic "nice" opening that tends to perform well in multi-round games.

My strategic reasoning for Round 5:

1. **Tit-for-Tat response**: The most effective strategy in iterated prisoner's dilemma games is often Tit-for-Tat - mirror the opponent's last move. My opponent defected in Round 4, so I should defect in Round 5. This signals that I won't be exploited without consequence.

2. **Deterrence**: If I cooperate again after being defected on, I signal that I'm a pushover who can be exploited repeatedly. This would invite further defection from my opponent.

3. **Risk assessment**: If my opponent cooperates in Round 5 and I defect, I get 5 (recovering some of my Round 4 loss). If my opponent defects again and I defect, we both get 1 - better than the 0 I'd get if I cooperated and they defected again.

4. **Expected value**: Defecting dominates cooperating in Round 5 given the opponent's Round 4 behavior. If they cooperate: defect gives 5 vs cooperate gives 3. If they defect: defect gives 1 vs cooperate gives 0.

5. **Signaling for future rounds**: By defecting now, I communicate that I use a reciprocal strategy, which may encourage the opponent to return to mutual cooperation in subsequent rounds for mutual benefit.

The defection in Round 4 broke the cooperative equilibrium, and I must respond accordingly to protect my cumulative score.

      - RandomBot chose 'defect'
        Reasoning: Random bot chose defect.
      - ClaudeSonnet4_6 chose 'cooperate'
        Reasoning: This is the first round with no history to go on. In a multi-round prisoner's dilemma, starting with cooperation is generally the strongest opening strategy for several reasons:

1. **Establishing a cooperative baseline**: If the opponent is also using a cooperative or tit-for-tat style strategy, mutual cooperation yields 3 points each round — far better than the mutual defection outcome of 1 point each.

2. **Signal of good faith**: Cooperating first signals willingness to work toward mutually beneficial outcomes. This can encourage the opponent to cooperate in return, setting up a positive cycle.

3. **Risk assessment**: Yes, if the opponent defects while I cooperate, I get 0 and they get 5. But this is one round of loss versus potentially many rounds of mutual 3-point gains if cooperation is established.

4. **Tit-for-tat logic**: The most successful strategies in iterated prisoner's dilemma tournaments (like Axelrod's) tend to start cooperative and then mirror the opponent's behavior. Starting cooperative keeps all options open.

5. **Unknown opponent**: Without any information about the opponent's strategy, cooperation is the best way to probe their intentions while leaving room for a productive long-term relationship.

The expected value of cooperation in round 1, assuming roughly equal probability of opponent cooperating or defecting, is reasonable, and the long-term upside of establishing mutual cooperation far outweighs the short-term risk.

My opponent has been a consistent cooperator for 9 straight rounds. After being betrayed in round 10, they face a decision: do they retaliate (defect) or continue cooperating? Many players using tit-for-tat or similar strategies would defect in round 11 as retaliation for my defection in round 10.

Key considerations:
1. If they defect (retaliation): My best response is also to defect (1 vs 0 if I cooperate)
2. If they cooperate (forgiveness): Defecting gives me 5, cooperating gives me 3

The expected value calculation:
- If I defect: I get either 5 (if they cooperate) or 1 (if they defect)
- If I cooperate: I get either 3 (if they cooperate) or 0 (if they defect)

Given that I just betrayed them, there's a significant probability they'll defect in retaliation. If the probability of them defecting is p:
- Expected value of defecting: 5(1-p) + 1(p) = 5 - 4p
- Expected value of cooperating: 3(1-p) + 0(p) = 3 - 3p

Defecting is better when: 5 - 4p > 3 - 3p → 2 > p → p < 2

This means defecting is always better regardless of p (since p is between 0 and 1). Defecting dominates cooperating in this round from a pure expected value standpoint.

Additionally, since I've already broken the cooperative equilibrium, the trust is damaged. Even if I cooperate now, they may still defect in retaliation, and then we'd be in a worse position. The cooperative streak is likely broken regardless.

I'll defect again to maximize my expected payoff this round.

================================================================================
                        LLM PROMPT TEMPLATE REFERENCE
================================================================================
Below is the prompt structure sent to LLM agents during the game:

[System Prompt Template]
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
You are agent '{agent_id}', playing a multi-round decision-making game with another player.
In each round, you and the other player must independently choose either 'cooperate' or 'defect'.
There is no communication other than seeing each other's choice history.

--- Payout Matrix ---
- You choose 'cooperate', Opponent chooses 'cooperate': You get R, Opponent gets R
- You choose 'defect', Opponent chooses 'defect': You get P, Opponent gets P
- You choose 'cooperate', Opponent chooses 'defect': You get S, Opponent gets T
- You choose 'defect', Opponent chooses 'cooperate': You get T, Opponent gets S

--- Goal ---
Maximize your own cumulative score across all rounds. Think strategically about how your choices impact future rounds.
[Optional: Custom Profile/Directives text defined in system_prompt]
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------

-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Current Round: {round_num}

--- Game History So Far ---
Round 1: You chose '{my_choice}', Opponent chose '{opponent_choice}'. Your payoff: X, Opponent payoff: Y.
Round 2: ...

Submit your choice for the current round ('cooperate' or 'defect').
